The #KennedyRetirement is only 24 hours old so I don't propose to have all the solutions worked out to this problem here, but it's a problem that should be getting more attention and thought to what the right solutions are. /8
In that sense, the mess of the Donald Trump presidency, which stems entirely from his own norm-breaking and potentially even law-breaking behavior, is now a colossal mess for our constitutional system. /7
That is simply not a viable scenario for a democracy rooted in the rule of law. It's a simple concept: the accused may not choose who will be judge in their case. /6
That could come via Trump being subpoenaed and fighting it, being indicted and fighting it, or even trying to pardon himself. All of those things, if they happen, would likely be resolved not just by the Supreme Court, but specifically by whoever Trump next appoints. /5
It's therefore not just possible, but I'd say it's likely that whoever Trump nominates may cast the decisive vote that determines whether Trump is civilly or criminally exposed on any of these things. /4
Yet that's exactly what might happen here. Trump is currently under investigation on a range of fronts, from whether he obstructed justice to whether he colluded with Russia to whether his pre-presidency business dealings were on the up and up. /3
But I cannot think of a prior example of a President appointing a justice who could rule on that President's personal exposure to serious criminal or civil liability. /2
A short THREAD on a huge issue: A major thing missing to me so far in the #KennedyRetirement coverage is this: all Presidents who appoint justices might have those justices rule on their official acts, like policy initiatives or legislation. /1

It‚Äôs critical that the media, public and Congress not buy it. As both the underlying theory is wrong, and even if it were right, it only means if Trump did obstruct, he should be impeached. /21
The deliberate leak of this memo is part of what will be a concerted public campaign to make those fudges and try to win over the court of public opinion. /20
And any sound lawyer would agree they are criminally chargeable, at a minimum after a president leaves office, if not before. /19
If, on the other hand, the President told a witness to lie, or dangled a pardon to induce a witness to obstruct, those are not Article II powers under any theory. /18
The other way the Trump team is pulling a fast one is that the theory, even if one bought it, would only apply to the President shutting down an investigation. /17
But that‚Äôs not what the theory actually holds.  Trump‚Äôs lawyers think they can fudge not being chargeable and not being impeachable as the same thing. They're not. /16
But the Trump lawyers plan to spin the theory to say that it means not only can Trump not be criminally charged, but that there‚Äôs actually nothing inherently wrong with him obstructing and thus it‚Äôs not a grounds for impeachment either. /15
In fact, the unitary executive theory is actually precisely that the president can end any investigation and if he abuses that power, the remedy is impeachment. /14
It would say nothing about whether the President can be impeached for the same acts. /13
But here‚Äôs the rub: that unitary executive theory would only extend to saying the President can‚Äôt be charged with the crime of obstruction for trying to end an investigation. /12
And so the Trump team expects to get support from conservative legal scholars on this. /11
That theory argues that the President can do basically what he wants with the Justice Dept because all executive power, including the power to open or close an investigation, ultimately emanates from the President. /10
But here‚Äôs the other tricky thing the Trump team is trying to do. They know that some conservative legal scholars believe in something called the ‚ÄúUnitary Executive‚Äù theory. /9
Here, if he were to interfere with an investigation in order to protect himself, his family, or his aides, that‚Äôs not a ‚Äúfaithful execution‚Äù and therefore is beyond his constitutional executive powers. /8
In this case, it means the President has to execute the laws ‚Äúin good faith‚Äù ‚Äì in other words, to advance the public interest and not some corrupt, self-interested purpose. /7
The word ‚Äúfaithfully‚Äù there has to mean something as constitutional interpretation requires every word to be there for a reason. /6 See also this brief by @preetbharara @johnwdean @joycewhitevance et al https://t.co/5bWRSeA81j
As @protctdemocracy has explained at length, among other reasons, the Constitution charges POTUS to ‚Äúfaithfully execute‚Äù the law. /5 https://t.co/vhTWoqmSXI
There are 2 problems with this theory. The 1st is that it‚Äôs wrong. /4 https://t.co/ehusvK22zV
Trump floated this theory himself a few months back when he claimed to have ‚Äúan absolute right‚Äù to do whatever he wants with the Justice Dept. /3
The Trump lawyers are trying to pull a fast one in a few ways. First, they‚Äôre trying to say Trump can‚Äôt commit the crime of obstruction of justice because he runs the justice department and can do whatever he wants with it. /2
